Socially BeneÔ¨Ācial Rationality:
The Value of Strategic Farmers, Social Entrepreneurs and
For-ProÔ¨Āt Firms in Crop Planting Decisions
Ming Hu
Rotman School of Management, University of Toronto, Toronto, Ontario, Canada M5S 3E6, ming.hu@rotman.utoronto.ca
Yan Liu
College of Management and Economics, Tianjin University, Tianjin, China, yan.liu@tju.edu.cn
Wenbin Wang
School of International Business Administration, Shanghai University of Finance and Economics, Shanghai, China 200433,
wang.wenbin@shufe.edu.cn
September 24, 2017
Price Ô¨āuctuations in agricultural markets are an obstacle to poverty reduction for small-scale farmers in
developing countries. We build a micro-foundation to study how farmers of heterogeneous production costs,
under price Ô¨āuctuations, make crop-planting decisions over time to maximize their incomes. We consider both
strategic farmers, who rationally anticipate the near-future prices as a basis for making planting decisions,
and na¬®ńĪve farmers, who shortsightedly react to recent crop prices. The latter behavior may cause recurring
over- or under-production, which leads to price Ô¨āuctuations. We Ô¨Ānd it important to cultivate a suÔ¨Écient
number of strategic farmers, because their self-interested behavior alone, made possible by suÔ¨Écient market
information, can reduce price volatility and beneÔ¨Āt all focal farmers. In the absence of strategic farmers,
a well-designed pre-season procurement contract, oÔ¨Äered by a social entrepreneur or a for-proÔ¨Āt Ô¨Ārm to a
fraction of contract farmers, brings beneÔ¨Āt to all focal farmers as well as to the Ô¨Ārm itself. More strikingly, the
contract not only brings equity in the long run among farmers of the same production cost, but also reduces
income disparity over time among farmers of heterogeneous costs, regardless of whether they are contract
farmers or not. On the other hand, a non-socially-optimal contract may reÔ¨āect a social entrepreneur‚Äôs over-
subsidy tendency, or a for-proÔ¨Āt Ô¨Ārm‚Äôs speculative incentive to mitigate but not eliminate the market price
Ô¨āuctuation, both preventing farmers from achieving the most welfare.
1.
Introduction
In developing countries, small-scale farmers are among the poorest, and they are faced with con-
siderable income uncertainty. One of the main obstacles to poverty reduction for them is the price
Ô¨āuctuation in agricultural markets. In China, for example, farmers who plant fruit or vegetables
(such as watermelon, oranges, loquat, gourds, cabbage, etc.) often go bankrupt after a rich harvest
1
 Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=2847477 
Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=2847477

2
because prices are slashed in local markets (China News 2014); and in India, small local onion and
potato growers suÔ¨Äered a more than 50% price drop from 2015 to 2016 (Sharma 2016, Buradikatti
2016). In fact, price Ô¨āuctuations, far from being rare, are widely observed in agricultural markets.
According to a report published jointly by a number of international organizations such as the
Food and Agriculture Organization, World Bank and World Trade Organization, agricultural mar-
kets are intrinsically subject to greater price variation than other markets (UNCTAD 2011). From
1983 to 1997 the market prices for Robusta coÔ¨Äee beans Ô¨āuctuated by 40 percent to 195 percent
(Oli Brown and Gibson 2008), and the prices of oranges in local Chinese markets (see Figure 1)
show evidence of strong cyclic annual price swings. As price Ô¨āuctuation is a signiÔ¨Ācant obstacle to
improvements in the welfare of small-scale farmers, in this paper we study strategies, enabled by
individuals or institutions, that can stabilize prices and increase small-scale farmers‚Äô welfare.

every year as oranges are perishable and farmers in China harvest them only once a year.
The explanation of perpetuating market price Ô¨āuctuations in the agricultural market has been
developed since the celebrated, macro-economic, cobweb theorem (Ezekiel 1938). That is, the pro-
duction is determined by the farmers‚Äô response to the price and farmers often make short-sighted
crop-planting decisions based on the relatively easily obtained prices of the previous season, or
blindly do what other farmers are doing (the latter action even further ampliÔ¨Āes that irrational-
ity). Bariyo (2014) reported farmers‚Äô back-and-forth decisions to abandon and then resume coÔ¨Äee
cultivation on the basis of the latest market prices. Making crop planting decisions in such a way
is easy for farmers and seems logical to them, but it ignores the interaction between crop supply
and the resulting price in the market, and therefore often leads to price Ô¨āuctuations. Here are two
recent examples that manifest the cobweb phenomenon.
 Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=2847477 
Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=2847477

3
Example 1. The unprecedented high market price of apples before 2015 induced some farm-
ers in China to expand their planting areas. The resulting overproduction led to Ô¨Āerce market
competition and a signiÔ¨Ācant price drop in 2015 (China National Radio 2015).
Example 2. The high prices of other staples have encouraged many small farmers in Brazil to
stop planting beans, which led to the bean shortage that has sent bean prices skyrocketing in 2016
(Parkin and Lewis 2016).
In both examples, farmers who follow the latest market price suÔ¨Äer a welfare loss later due to
the overproduction of the crops they plant, and they miss the chance to plant other crops that
would have been more proÔ¨Ātable. The extant literature on the cobweb phenomenon often takes the
supply and demand curves as exogenously given. However, these curves are determined by farmers‚Äô
individual incentives and planting decisions. To study welfare improving mechanisms, we start
with individual farmer‚Äôs utility maximization problem. We Ô¨Ārst show how farmers‚Äô (involuntarily)
short-sighted behavior leads to a recurring boom-and-bust, which provides a micro-foundation for
the cobweb phenomenon. Then we further study sustainable and incentive-compatible mechanisms
that are applied to a fraction of farmers but can help other farmers increase their welfare.
The recurring slashing of prices and loss in farmers‚Äô welfare are attributed largely to farmers‚Äô
diÔ¨Éculty in obtaining market information, predicting future prices and assessing the eÔ¨Äect of their
planting decisions on themselves. There have been repeated eÔ¨Äorts by governments, market institu-
tions, and non-proÔ¨Āt organizations (NGOs) to prevent farmers from behaving short-sightedly. For
instance, governments, NGOs, and business sectors use information and communication technol-
ogy to disseminate information, such as historical and current market prices, weather information
and advisories about near-future market prices, to farmers (see examples in Chen and Tang 2015).
Such practices, however, may not be very eÔ¨Äective since not all farmers can obtain or process the
market information, partly due to the limited access to education in developing countries (Epstein
and Yuthas 2012). It is very likely that only a small fraction of farmers are able to use the market
information to predict the near-future market prices and make planting decisions that maximize
their individual welfare. Since the farmers who can do so are self-serving, it is not clear whether
their decisions also improve the welfare of the other farmers or make it even worse.
We begin our analysis by building a stylized multi-period model that captures the involuntarily
irrational behavior of farmers who base their planting decisions on the previous season‚Äôs market
price. Building on this model, we study the impact of those self-serving, forward-looking farmers,
referred to as strategic farmers, on the welfare of the other farmers who are short-sighted, referred
to as na¬®ńĪve farmers. We Ô¨Ānd that the strategic farmers‚Äô ability to predict the near-future market
 Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=2847477 
Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=2847477

4
price can help stabilize the market price, thereby beneÔ¨Āting all farmers who have no production
cost advantage, hereafter, referred to as focal farmers. This implies that the strategic farmers‚Äô
forward-looking behavior can be both self-serving and socially beneÔ¨Ācial. On the other hand, we
show that the beneÔ¨Āts are greatly discounted when the number of strategic farmers is small or
when na¬®ńĪve farmers have limited savings and would exit farming permanently due to bankruptcy.
Therefore it is imperative to explore other solutions.
Contract farming is another way to oÔ¨Äer farmers a guaranteed market outlet and reduce their
welfare uncertainty. There is a pre-season procurement contract between the farmers and the
buyer, with a speciÔ¨Āc procurement price and date for near-future transactions. In practice most
of the contract farming projects are initiated by social entrepreneurs (SEs) and for-proÔ¨Āt Ô¨Ārms.
For instance, Starbucks has contract-farming agreements with local farmers who plant coÔ¨Äee beans
in Thailand and Indonesia (Rungfapaisarn 2013). Unlike government and NGOs who give free
aids to the poor, for-proÔ¨Āt Ô¨Ārms have proÔ¨Āt-making goals and SEs balance for-proÔ¨Āt goals with
social objectives. It is reported that many SEs in the agricultural business expect the procurement
contracts to Ô¨Āght poverty and guarantee a reliable supply of agricultural products (Fraser 2012).
For a SE, we are interested in identifying a contract that both improves farmers‚Äô welfare and brings
enough proÔ¨Āt to sustain its own operations. For a proÔ¨Āt-driven Ô¨Ārm, we focus on a setting when
the goal of proÔ¨Āt maximization is compatible with improving farmers‚Äô welfare.
SpeciÔ¨Ācally, building on the micro-foundation of farmers‚Äô utility maximization, we study farming
contracts between farmers with heterogeneous production costs and a Ô¨Ārm (SE or proÔ¨Āt-driven
enterprise) that oÔ¨Äers a constant procurement price to a fraction of farmers. We Ô¨Ānd that there
exists a unique price under which both farmers and the SE beneÔ¨Āt. This price depends on the
potential market size and farmers‚Äô production costs. A lower price (i.e., insuÔ¨Écient subsidy) does
not alleviate the market Ô¨āuctuation, while a higher price (i.e., over-subsidy) distorts the market
and hurts both the Ô¨Ārm and some farmers. Moreover, we Ô¨Ānd that the optimal contract not only
brings equity in the long run among farmers of the same production cost, but also reduces income
disparity over time among farmers of heterogeneous costs, regardless of whether they are contract
farmers or not. This Ô¨Ānding highlights the additional beneÔ¨Āt of the win-win contract in creating
fairness. When extending the contract form to time-varying procurement prices, we show that the
prices in the optimal time-varying contracts converge to the same unique price.
The contract design for a for-proÔ¨Āt Ô¨Ārm, however, is more subtle. In a setting where a for-proÔ¨Āt
Ô¨Ārm has to pay a high price to source from an external market if not satisÔ¨Āed by contract farmers,
the above optimally-designed contract for the SE also increases the for-proÔ¨Āt Ô¨Ārm‚Äôs proÔ¨Āt and
 Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=2847477 
Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=2847477

5
meanwhile improves farmers‚Äô welfare. But it does not always maximize the Ô¨Ārm‚Äôs proÔ¨Āt. It is likely
that the for-proÔ¨Āt Ô¨Ārm has a speculative incentive to mitigate but not completely eliminate the
market price Ô¨āuctuation. In such a case, the for-proÔ¨Āt Ô¨Ārm alternates sourcing between contract
farmers and the external market to enjoy the most cost eÔ¨Éciency, preventing farmers from achieving
their most welfare. Therefore, regulation on for-proÔ¨Āt Ô¨Ārms may be needed.
This paper contributes to the literature that addresses the challenges of reducing agricultural
market Ô¨āuctuations and improving farmers‚Äô welfare in developing economies. SpeciÔ¨Ācally, we Ô¨Ārst
build a micro-foundation of farmers‚Äô utility maximization, which enables a detailed welfare analy-
sis. Second, with the micro-foundation, we study interactions among farmers with heterogeneous
production costs in jointly determining the crop‚Äôs price, which in turn aÔ¨Äects their welfare. We
show that self-interested behavior by individual farmers can be socially beneÔ¨Ācial. Third, we design
procurement contracts for social entrepreneurs or proÔ¨Āt-driven Ô¨Ārms that are oÔ¨Äered to a fraction
of farmers and are win-win for both the organization and all focal farmers. In sum, our results
demonstrate that self-interested behavior by individual farmers, social entrepreneurs, or proÔ¨Āt-
driven Ô¨Ārms can be socially beneÔ¨Ācial at the same time, beneÔ¨Āting all focal stakeholders, with
carefully disclosed information or optimally-designed contracts oÔ¨Äered to a fraction of farmers.
2.
Literature Review
Our paper is closely related to the works on the cobweb phenomenon in the macro-economics and
agronomics literature. Ezekiel (1938) Ô¨Ārst attributes the phenomenon (recurring cyclical herding in
production) to the fact that producers often base their short-run production plans on the assump-
tion that the present prices will continue. Our paper diÔ¨Äers from that literature in three ways. First,
our goal is to illustrate that the self-interested behavior of strategic farmers, social entrepreneur
and for-proÔ¨Āt Ô¨Ārms can reduce the price Ô¨āuctuations, whereas the focus of the cobweb theory is to
explain the price oscillations observed in various markets. Notably, we take the perspective that
such price oscillations can be mitigated by carefully designed, incentive-compatible mechanisms.
Second, one key feature of our model is that we build a micro-foundation of an individual
farmer utility model with heterogeneous production costs, such that the supply curve is derived
from farmers‚Äô utility maximization rather than being exogenously assumed as it is in the cobweb
models. With such a utility-based model, we can also evaluate each individual farmer‚Äôs welfare.
For example, we show that a carefully designed contract oÔ¨Äered to a fraction of farmers beneÔ¨Āts
all focal stakeholders and, at the same time, reduces income disparity among farmers.
Third, the cobweb models lead to the discussion about the rational expectations assumption and
its validity for all farmers (see, e.g., Nerlove 1958). This assumption is often made in the operations
 Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=2847477 
Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=2847477

6
literature on strategic consumers (see, e.g., Su 2010, Cachon and Swinney 2011 and references
therein). We consider a fraction of farmers who have rational expectations or who contract with
social entrepreneurs or for-proÔ¨Āt Ô¨Ārms. This feature emphasizes interactions via the market prices
among farmers who have diÔ¨Äerent rationality or access to external farming contracts. In a diÔ¨Äerent
context but a similar spirit, Su (2010) and Cachon and Swinney (2009) consider the interactions
between myopic and forward-looking customers, and Hu et al. (2017) consider the interactions
among customers who have diÔ¨Äerent granularity of information about the service system congestion.
The latter shows that in the presence of information cost, a fraction of customers may intentionally
ignore the real-time information, thereby beneÔ¨Āting social welfare. AÔ¨āaki et al. (2015) Ô¨Ānd that in
the presence of hassle cost, a fraction of forward-looking customers may intentionally choose not
to search for more information and this rational ignorant behavior may beneÔ¨Āt the society.
We also contribute to the economics and operations management literature on ‚Äúsocial herding,‚ÄĚ
as our model captures the farmers‚Äô shortsighted herding behavior in planting or abandoning crops.
Veeraraghavan and Debo (2009) identify the herding eÔ¨Äect in queues where consumers infer service
quality from the length of the queue. In our model, herding is an outcome of farmers‚Äô shortsighted
reactions to recent prices, and we study the role of self-serving strategic farmers and Ô¨Ārms in
alleviating the herding in crop planting. We provide a solution under which the herding eÔ¨Äect can
be eliminated by oÔ¨Äering a carefully designed contract, which beneÔ¨Āts both farmers and Ô¨Ārms.
Notably as opposed to the social herding eÔ¨Äect, Tereyagoglu and Veeraraghavan (2012), Agrawal
et al. (2016) and Momot et al. (2016) consider consumers‚Äô purchasing behavior that depends on
their intrinsic desire for exclusivity. More broadly, this paper is related to this emerging literature
on social operations management, which focuses on how social interactions leading to collective
social behaviors (in this paper, individual farmers‚Äô planting decisions collectively determine the
total production quantity and market price), have an impact on Ô¨Ārms‚Äô operational decisions (in this
paper, procurement), and how operational decisions (in this paper, contract farming) can inÔ¨āuence
the formation of collective social behaviors.
Our study also links to the literature showing that a lack of information can lead to endogenized
uncertainty in business practices. For example, the well-known ‚Äúbullwhip eÔ¨Äect‚ÄĚ (see Lee et al.
1997) shows that lack of the information about the true customer demand causes the variance
of order quantities to increase as one moves upstream along the supply chain. Hu et al. (2015)
show that sales uncertainty can occur when customers are more inÔ¨āuenced by others when making
purchasing decisions. Cooper et al. (2006) show that incorrect beliefs about customer behavior
cause a spiral-down eÔ¨Äect, i.e., revenues systematically decrease over time. In contrast, we focus
 Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=2847477 
Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=2847477

7
on investigating win-win mechanisms that can be implemented by informed individuals, a social
entrepreneur or a for-proÔ¨Āt Ô¨Ārm, to reduce the negative eÔ¨Äects of the lack of market information.
Poverty alleviation in developing countries has been studied in the burgeoning operations man-
agement literature that focuses on the design of socially responsible operations (see Sodhi and
Tang 2014 for a comprehensive review). Many of those studies explore the value of disclosing mar-
ket information in helping farmers improve their welfare. For instance, Chen et al. (2013) study
Indian conglomerate ITC‚Äôs new business model, where the Ô¨Ārm helps farmers to obtain various
information through E-Choupals network and allows farmers to sell directly to the Ô¨Ārm. Chen
and Tang (2015) examine the value of both private and public signals of the agricultural market.
Chen et al. (2015) investigate the incentives for knowledge sharing among competing farmers. Tang
et al. (2015) examine whether under a Cournot competition, farmers should utilize market infor-
mation to optimize their production plans when both the market potential and the process yield
are uncertain. Belloni et al. (2016) show that a monopsonistic buyer (a downstream principal in
general) with private demand information can contract with segmented farmers (upstream agents)
to restore the Ô¨Ārst-best productive eÔ¨Éciency. Our paper diÔ¨Äers from those papers in that we focus
on the scenario when the market information is not available to all the farmers. We consider some
farmers that can only react shortsightedly to the market, and study how these farmers‚Äô welfare is
aÔ¨Äected by self-serving strategic farmers and Ô¨Ārms. By modeling farmers‚Äô dynamic crop planting
decisions over time, we Ô¨Ānd that the self-serving individuals and Ô¨Ārms can also be beneÔ¨Ācial to
all focal farmers. In terms of methodology, we adopt a multi-period model with interactions both
within and across periods, whereas all the aforementioned papers build a one-period model.
Finally, our paper is also related to the literature on price protection mechanisms. Nicholls
and Opal (2005) review price protection implemented through fair trade operations and study
its eÔ¨Äectiveness. Haight (2011) suggests that a fair-trade certiÔ¨Ācation model, which guarantees a
Ô¨Āxed price if the farmers meet high production standards, can be poorly implemented and only
applied to limited types of crops (Fair Trade USA 2012). Annan and Schlenker (2015) study the
eÔ¨Äectiveness of federal crop insurance in the U.S. They argue that this government program may
be a disincentive for farmers to adapt to extreme weather. Most of the papers referred to above
are empirical studies. In our paper, we develop an analytical model to study the eÔ¨Äectiveness
of a pre-season procurement contract, oÔ¨Äered by an SE or a for-proÔ¨Āt Ô¨Ārm, in reducing market
Ô¨āuctuation and improving farmers‚Äô welfare. We identify many potential risks associated with the
contract and Ô¨Ānd that a careful design is crucial to the eÔ¨Äectiveness of such a contract. Federgruen
et al. (2016) consider a manufacturer that oÔ¨Äers a menu of contracts to a set of contract farmers.
 Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=2847477 
Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=2847477

8
They focus on information asymmetry in farmers‚Äô characteristics and develop eÔ¨Écient approaches
to designing the contracts. Mu et al. (2015) consider intermediaries who compete in collecting milk
from farmers. They Ô¨Ānd competition can either hurt or improve the quality of the milk, depending
on quality test mechanism. Lee et al. (2000) study the price adjustment mechanism that aims to
reduce price uncertainty but they study an entirely diÔ¨Äerent setting.
3.
Model
We study a multi-period model in which farmers make planting decisions for a single crop in each
period over an inÔ¨Ānite horizon with no time discounting. The individual farmers are inÔ¨Ānitesimally
small-scale, and the size of the farmer population is normalized to 1. At the beginning of period t,
each farmer observes pt‚ąí1, the crop‚Äôs market price in the end of previous period t ‚ąí1 and decides
whether or not to cultivate the crop in the current period t. The planting decision depends on each
farmer‚Äôs individual assessment of the near-future crop price and the cost of planting the crop. The
crop production takes one full period. At the end of the period, all of the harvested crop is sold to
the market with the price pt determined by the total output from all farmers.
Farmers are heterogeneous in their capability of assessing crop prices. We assume a fraction, őĪ,
of the farmers is strategic and the remaining is na¬®ńĪve. SpeciÔ¨Ācally, the strategic farmers have full
information about the market and can take rational expectation to predict the near-future market
price pt, but na¬®ńĪve farmers are incapable of correctly predicting the market price and they simply
take pt‚ąí1 from the last period as the indicator of the near-future price at the end of period t.
Besides, farmers also have heterogeneous endowment of production costs, denoted as c, which is
uniformly distributed on [0, ¬Įc] capturing the idiosyncratic production capabilities among farmers.
Let F denote the cumulative distribution function (c.d.f.) of production cost c. We model a farmers‚Äô
perceived utility of planting the crop as the farmer‚Äôs individual assessed market price minus its
production cost. That is, a strategic farmer‚Äôs perceived utility, denoted by us
t, and a na¬®ńĪve farmer‚Äôs

In the beginning of each period, a farmer chooses to cultivate the crop if and only if his perceived
utility is no less than zero. Our model could accommodate the heterogeneity in farmers‚Äô outside
options as well, by interpreting c as a general cost including farmers‚Äô opportunity cost of farming.

In what follows, we will Ô¨Ānd the connection between farmers‚Äô na¬®ńĪve behavior and cyclical market

Assumption 4 (Divergence by Default). b ‚Č•¬Įc.
More importantly, our model provides a micro-foundation of farmers‚Äô utility maximization for
the boom-and-bust cobweb phenomenon (see Ezekiel 1938): When the price realized at the end of
a period is high, a large number of na¬®ńĪve farmers Ô¨āood to plant the crop in the upcoming period
because they think the price will continue to be high. This results in crop over-production and a
price drop in the following period. Similarly, when the price realized at the end of a period is low,
na¬®ńĪve farmers choose to abandon planting the crop (they may plant another crop, see Section 6.1,
or seek other outside options, such as going to the city as migrant workers), thus leading to a supply
 Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=2847477 
Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=2847477

shortage and a price rebound in the following period. The na¬®ńĪve farmers get trapped in the vicious
cycle and suÔ¨Äer from the price Ô¨āuctuations caused by the recurring under- or over-production. This
unfavorable situation is persistent when there is a lack of suÔ¨Écient strategic farmers, i.e., őĪ ‚Č§b‚ąí¬Įc
2b
(equivalently, g(őĪ) ‚Č•1), and will also be manifested in the following farmers‚Äô welfare analysis.

otherwise. Let market cycle i represent a pair of consecutive periods 2i‚ąí1 and 2i. We call a farmer‚Äôs

The micro-foundation enables a detailed welfare analysis for na¬®ńĪve farmers under price Ô¨āuctua-

planting the crop when the latest crop price is high and abandoning the crop when the price is
low. Their individual welfare is negative as shown in Proposition 2(b).
Unlike na¬®ńĪve farmers who have welfare loss because they unconsciously yet eÔ¨Äectively ‚Äúherd‚ÄĚ
in making planting decisions, strategic farmers can rationally anticipate market prices and avoid
herding with na¬®ńĪve farmers. When there is a suÔ¨Éciently large fraction of strategic farmers (i.e.,
g(őĪ) < 1, or equivalently, őĪ > b‚ąí¬Įc
2b ), their self-serving planting decisions always help stabilize market
prices in the long run, and may improve na¬®ńĪve farmers‚Äô welfare at the same time. The following
proposition conÔ¨Ārms the role of strategic farmers in enhancing farmers‚Äô welfare.
Proposition 3 (Socially Beneficial Rationality). Suppose Assumptions 1-4 hold and
g(őĪ) < 1. Denote pn
2i as the lower price in any cycle i when all farmers are na¬®ńĪve farmers.
(i) In the short run (i.e., in each cycle i): (a) Each strategic farmer obtains a (weakly) higher
surplus than the na¬®ńĪve farmer with the same production cost; (b) Those na¬®ńĪve farmers who have
a production cost that is not too low, i.e., c > pn
2i‚ąí2, obtain a (weakly) higher surplus than that
without strategic farmers.
(ii) In the long run (i.e., as the cycle i approaches ‚ąě): (a) Each strategic farmer obtains the same
surplus as the na¬®ńĪve farmer with the same production cost; (b) Those na¬®ńĪve farmers who have a
production cost that is not too low, i.e., c > limi‚Üí‚ąěpn
2i, obtain a (weakly) higher surplus than that
without strategic farmers.
Proposition 3 shows that in both short and long run, the strategic farmers‚Äô forward-looking
behavior is indeed rational (part (a)), and such rational behavior is also socially beneÔ¨Ācial and
can help increase the welfare of na¬®ńĪve farmers (part (b)). The welfare improvement for na¬®ńĪve
farmers comes from the stabilized market price due to the presence of strategic farmers. The welfare
improvement is unambiguous for those farmers with not too low production cost, because their
planting decision is indeed aÔ¨Äected by the Ô¨āuctuating market prices.
The na¬®ńĪve farmers with very low production cost, however, might obtain lower, but still positive,
welfare in the presence of strategic farmers. This is because these farmers plant in all periods and
the average price when the market price process diverges (see Proposition 1(ii-b)), could be higher
 Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=2847477 
Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=2847477

14
than the limiting market price ¬Įp when the market price process converges. Through numerical
experiments we Ô¨Ānd that in most cases this welfare decrease is relatively small in comparison with
the welfare gain the other farmers obtain, and thus the total welfare of all farmers tends to be
improved by the presence of strategic farmers (we provide a suÔ¨Écient condition of this result in
Part (2c) of the proof of Proposition 3 in Online Appendix E). Since these farmers with very low
costs always obtain positive surplus (Proposition 2(a)) and is not signiÔ¨Ācantly aÔ¨Äected by strategic
farmers, for the remaining of the paper, our analysis will mostly focus on those focal farmers who
do not have a production cost advantage (i.e., with a production cost that is not too low). More
speciÔ¨Ācally, we focus on farmers with a production cost that is no smaller than a threshold, denoted
as c, which is equal to the lower market price in each cycle over the long run when there are naive
farmers only (i.e., c = p0 for b = ¬Įc; c = ‚Ą¶‚ąíb for b > ¬Įc, see p2i in Proposition 1 part (ii) with őĪ = 0).
Although there is beneÔ¨Āt in being forward-looking, cultivating a suÔ¨Écient number of strategic
farmers is still not easy. Farmers may lack the intrinsic reasoning ability or the external aids
to obtain full market information and may thus fail to make fully rational decisions. In Online
Appendix B, we discuss the impact of backward-looking strategic behavior on the market dynamics.
In addition, the size of strategic farmers aÔ¨Äects the convergence rate of the market price process.
Corollary 2. Suppose Assumptions 1-4 hold and g(őĪ) < 1. The fraction of strategic farmers
őĪ aÔ¨Äects only the convergence rate of the market price process but not the limiting market price.
The larger the fraction of strategic farmers őĪ is, the faster the market price process converges.
A relatively smaller őĪ would result in a slower price convergence and a larger loss for some na¬®ńĪve
farmers before the market price is stabilized. If a farmer‚Äôs loss exceeds his capital or savings, he
may go bankrupt and quit the farming business, which is considered in the following.
Proposition 4 (Impact of Bankruptcy). Suppose Assumptions 1-4 hold and those farmers
who exit the market due to bankruptcy never come back. Then even when there is no strategic
farmer at all (i.e., őĪ = 0) and the market price process would diverge without bankruptcy (i.e.,
b ‚Č•¬Įc), the market price process still converges, but to a limiting market price that is higher than ¬Įp.
When farmers‚Äô savings are limited, Proposition 4 shows the negative impacts of bankruptcy
on the society: the collapse of farmers who would have survived with proper interferences and a
shortage of supply in the long run resulting in a high limiting market price. This situation may
beneÔ¨Āt those farmers who have cost advantage and remain in the market, but it is at the deadly
cost of those farmers who go bankrupt and results in surplus loss for crop buyers. One can naturally
imagine that the above situation still hold when there is an insuÔ¨Écient number of strategic farmers.
 Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=2847477 
Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=2847477

15
Therefore, it is imperative to examine other options that hold the potential to timely stabilize the
market price and fairly distribute the beneÔ¨Āts of price stabilization.
4.
Social Entrepreneurs
In the last section, we showed that the conversion of a suÔ¨Écient fraction of na¬®ńĪve farmers to strate-
gic farmers would tilt the market dynamics from Ô¨āuctuation to stabilization. However, one often
observes that it is very diÔ¨Écult to cultivate enough strategic farmers for several reasons. First, in
the case of fresh, local specialty fruits and vegetables, there is often a lack of extensive advisory
systems that would help farmers obtain the market information about each crop and track the
activities of other farmers. Second, farmers‚Äô limited ability to predict crop prices and their short-
sighted behavior have remained major obstacles despite considerable advances in crop advisory
communications. Moreover, external factors such as market uncertainty may add signiÔ¨Ācant com-
plexity to the market stabilizing eÔ¨Äorts, as we will discuss later in Section 6.2. For those reasons,
in many developing countries where those problems are prevalent, it may not be as eÔ¨Äective as it
sounds to rely purely on farmers‚Äô forward-looking behavior to stabilize the market. One remedy
can be market stabilization eÔ¨Äorts by social organizations operating in a sustainable way.
In this section, we consider a social entrepreneur (SE) who aims at improving the social welfare
but also bears in mind sustaining his own operations, when na¬®ńĪve farmers do not voluntarily become
strategic. The SE oÔ¨Äers a fraction of farmers a pre-season procurement contract that buys out
all of their crop. We refer to this contract as a buyout contract. In this contract, SE selects and
announces a Ô¨Āxed buyout price pb for all periods (see Section 4.3.3 for time-varying buyout prices).
At the beginning of each period each farmer who is oÔ¨Äered the contract decides whether to sign
the contract with the SE. Farmers who accepted the SE‚Äôs contract at the beginning of period
t sell their crops to the SE at the price pb at the end of period t. Right after the SE buys the
crops from the farmers, he sells them in the market at the market price of that period, pt. The
pre-season buyout contract we study here is diÔ¨Äerent from the minimum-price support program
oÔ¨Äered by an NGO or government, in terms of the timing of sales commitment. In the latter, the
farmers contract with the NGO or government and can decide to sell their crop back to them
when market prices fall below a pre-speciÔ¨Āed level after the harvest, while our contract is agreed
on before the season starts. The pre-season contract can help guide the planting decisions in the
Ô¨Ārst place, whereas the minimum-price support program may have a delayed eÔ¨Äect. Though the
minimum-price support program can also guarantee farmers‚Äô payoÔ¨Äs, it very likely comes at the
cost of NGOs or governments, which may not be sustainable (see, e.g., Craymer 2016). Annan and
 Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=2847477 
Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=2847477

16
Schlenker (2015) consider moral hazard issues in minimum-price support programs, i.e., insurance
protection reduces individual eÔ¨Äorts in Ô¨Āghting against extreme weather conditions. We assume
away such issues in the buyout program, as we focus on the impact of price guarantee on individual
planting decisions. In the following subsection we will examine whether the aid from the SE would
bring sustainable beneÔ¨Āts to all farmers and to the SE himself as well.
4.1.
Market Equilibrium in the Presence of SE
We assume that all farmers are na¬®ńĪve and only an exogenous, őĪ, fraction of farmers have access
to the SE‚Äôs contract. The limited access could be attributed to many factors. For instance, some
farmers in rural areas may be too remote to be reached and others may decline to work with an
unfamiliar SE. We refer to farmers who are oÔ¨Äered the SE‚Äôs contract as type-S farmers and those
who are not as type-N farmers. A type-S farmer‚Äôs perceived utility, if he accepts the contract and
plants the crop, is pb ‚ąíc. Since the farmer would otherwise still behave na¬®ńĪvely and perceive utility
pt‚ąí1 ‚ąíc, he will accept the contract only if the oÔ¨Äered price pb is strictly higher than the market
price pt‚ąí1 in the last period. Hence, a type-S farmer‚Äôs perceived utility in period t, denoted by ub

Assumption 5. ‚Ą¶‚ąíb ‚Č§pb ‚Č§¬Įc.
We now study the long-term market trends for a certain level of buyout price pb. According to
Proposition 1 and Corollary 1, when b ‚Č•¬Įc and there are neither strategic farmers nor any contract
oÔ¨Äered by the SE, the market price oscillates over time, around the limiting market price ¬Įp. The
SE‚Äôs contract would stimulate planting the crop when the most recent market price is below pb
and thus reduce the price Ô¨āuctuation. The following proposition shows that the extent to which
the SE‚Äôs buyout contract can help reduce the price Ô¨āuctuation varies, depending on the buyout
price pb and the fraction of contract farmers őĪ.
Proposition 5. Suppose Assumptions 1-5 hold. Denote őĪ0 := 1 ‚ąí(pb‚ąí¬Įp)(¬Įc+b)

(ii) Suppose pb = ¬Įp. If őĪ > őĪ2, the price process converges to ¬Įp. Otherwise, it does not converge.
(iii) Suppose pb < ¬Įp. The price process does not converge.
When the buyout price pb is greater than the limiting market price ¬Įp, the market price process
would converge if őĪ > max{őĪ1,min{őĪ0,őĪ2}}, i.e., there is a suÔ¨Écient fraction of type-S farmers,
and would diverge if őĪ ‚Č§őĪ1. In fact, numerical experiments show that there exists a threshold
on őĪ in the range [őĪ1,max{őĪ1,min{őĪ0,őĪ2}}] such that when őĪ is greater than the threshold the
market price process converges to a value that is strictly less than ¬Įp, and when őĪ is less than that
threshold the process diverges. When pb is exactly equal to ¬Įp, the threshold on őĪ that determines
whether the process converges is őĪ2. For a buyout price pb that is below ¬Įp, the SE‚Äôs contract does
not stabilize the crop price. This is because, even if there is a large number of type-S farmers,
by the time the market price Ô¨āuctuates above pb no farmers would accept the buyout contract,
and thus the contract is no longer eÔ¨Äective and the price will not continue to converge. In such
situation, farmers are still exposed to signiÔ¨Ācant price risks.
When the buyout price is high (i.e., pb > ¬Įp) and provided to a suÔ¨Écient number of farmers, the
SE incurs losses from his operations in the long run since the market price is strictly less than
the buyout price ¬Įp. Even worse, some na¬®ńĪve farmers who are not oÔ¨Äered the contract (i.e., type-N
farmers) may give up farming in such a situation.
Corollary 3. Suppose Assumptions 1-5 hold. An inadequate subsidy (i.e., low buyout price)
does not eliminate the Ô¨āuctuations in the market price. Too large a subsidy (i.e., a high buyout
price), however, may create huge distortions in the market, causing many non-contract farmers to
give up farming, and result in losses for the SE.
 Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=2847477 
Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=2847477

18
The reason behind the market distortion when pb is too high is that the market price in the long
run decreases in pb and thus a higher buyout price induces more type-S farmers to produce the crop;
more production leads to a lower market price, and crowds out more type-N farmers who are not
protected by the buyout contract. This unintended consequence is exempliÔ¨Āed by many charitable
programs whose unfair subsidies undermine the eÔ¨Äorts of those unsupported poor and thus may
end up with sustaining their poverty (see Miller 2014). Corollary 3 reveals that SE‚Äôs eÔ¨Äorts, which
are inspired by a desire to do good, do not necessarily result in a satisfactory outcome. A buyout
price that is too high beneÔ¨Āts contract farmers at the expense of non-contract farmers and may
actually create more poverty. This also provides a caveat for governments and NGOs that may
have more centralized power to oÔ¨Äer subsidies, perhaps in the name of charity. Therefore, it is
desirable to Ô¨Ānd the optimal design of the buyout contract.

Proposition 6 (Socially Beneficial Rationality). Suppose Assumptions 1-6 hold. pb‚ąó=
¬Įp is the unique optimal solution to SE‚Äôs problem (10), under which the market price process con-
verges to ¬Įp and:
(a) The SE receives a positive proÔ¨Āt over any Ô¨Ānite horizon and the proÔ¨Āt per market cycle dimin-
ishes to 0 in the limit.
(b) Both type-S and type-N farmers, with a production cost c > c, receive (weakly) higher welfare
in both the short- and long-run due to the market stabilization in the presence of SE.
Proposition 6 shows that among all the buyout prices it is only the price of ¬Įp that maximizes the
focal farmers‚Äô total welfare while keeping the SE‚Äôs proÔ¨Āt nonnegative for any period. In addition, the
improvement of farmers‚Äô total welfare is balanced. As shown in Proposition 6(b), at the individual
level, both type-S and type-N farmers achieve welfare improvement because of the SE‚Äôs contract.
Thus, by setting the buyout contract as pb = ¬Įp, there is a win-win-win situation for the SE, type-
S and type-N farmers. The following propositions present more detailed views of virtues of this
optimal buyout contract.

welfare gap between high- and low-cost farmers is smaller among type-S farmers than that among
type-N farmers, which is further smaller than that among farmers in the scenario where all farmers
are na¬®ńĪve. Therefore, SE helps not only improve the total welfare but also reduce the welfare gap
between the advantaged and disadvantaged farmers. That is, the distribution of beneÔ¨Āt also tends
to be fair across farmers with heterogeneous production costs (see Proposition 8), in addition to
the long-run fairness among farmers with the same production cost, regardless of whether they are
contract farmers or not (see Proposition 7).
4.3.
Contract Implementation and Discussion
4.3.1.
Default Risk. As shown in Part (3a) of the proof of Proposition 6 in Online Appendix
H, before the market price process converges, those farmers who are oÔ¨Äered the contract and have
a production cost less than ¬Įp may periodically observe the realized market price higher than the
contract price pb = ¬Įp at which they agreed to sell to the SE. They may realize that their average
welfare is lower than that if they plant the crop and sell it directly to the market. Thus, these
farmers may choose to default on the SE contract before the market stabilizes. However, we Ô¨Ānd
that this possible default has no impact on the crop supply and hence the market price process is
unchanged. This is because these low-cost farmers will plant the crop in every period regardless of
 Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=2847477 
Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=2847477

21
whether they decide to sell in the market or to the SE. Thus, contract price adjustment or rewards
in any other form to prevent these low-cost farmers from defaulting may not be necessary.
4.3.2.
Impact of őĪ. Similar to the result with strategic farmers (see Corollary 2), under the
optimal buyout contract, a larger size of the type-S farmers does not change the market converging
price but increases the market convergence rate. However, diÔ¨Äerent from the role of strategic
farmers, the SE‚Äôs contract is only eÔ¨Äective in one period of every market cycle, as it is executed by
farmers only when the market price is low. This leads to a slower market price convergence rate
than that under the forward-looking behavior of strategic farmers in Section 3. This Ô¨Ānding about
the convergence rate indicates that cultivating strategic farmers can be a more eÔ¨Äective strategy
over the long run than simply oÔ¨Äering na¬®ńĪve farmers a buyout contract. Formally, we have:
Proposition 9. Suppose Assumptions 1-6 hold and pb = ¬Įp. The fraction of type-S farmers, őĪ,
aÔ¨Äects only the convergence rate of the market price process but not the limiting market price. The
larger the fraction of type-S farmers is, the faster the market price process converges. Moreover,
given the same őĪ, the market price process with the SE‚Äôs contract converges more slowly than that
with strategic farmers.
It is widely acknowledged that approaching and convincing farmers to participate in the contract
farming can be time-consuming. One immediate implication of Proposition 9 is that SE still needs
to make the buyout contract accessible to as many farmers as possible if the budget permits. This
is because, a large number of contract farmers speeds up the price convergence process and thus
not only directly beneÔ¨Āts most contract farmers themselves but also indirectly beneÔ¨Āts all other
focal non-contract farmers by increasing their welfare within a short period of time.
4.3.3.
Dynamic Price Contract. Another approach that holds the potential in expediting
the price convergence is to oÔ¨Äer time-varying buyout price contract, though it is more complicated
to communicate and implement. Consider the following SE‚Äôs problem. Let pb := {pb

convergence to ¬Įp while the SE does not incur any loss at any time. For any given suÔ¨Éciently small

Proposition 11 assumes that SE is willing to sacriÔ¨Āce itself to do good, which may not always
be true in practice.
Besides its complexity and inconvenience in revising the contract terms, another potential risk
associated with a dynamic contract, as opposed to committing to a Ô¨Āxed buyout price, is that the
SE could be more speculative and abuse the contract when oÔ¨Äering it, in order to earn more proÔ¨Āts
in the short term. For instance, the SE could contract with farmers at a price even lower than ¬Įp but
strictly higher than pt‚ąí1 at the beginning of an odd period t and sell at a higher market price in
the end of that period. That could dramatically slow down the market convergence rate and result
in lower farmer welfare. Therefore, monitoring and regulating SE‚Äôs behavior can be necessary. We
will further elaborate the speculative incentive in the model with a for-proÔ¨Āt Ô¨Ārm in Section 5.
4.3.4.
Long-Term Contract. Consider that the SE oÔ¨Äers type-S farmers a long-term con-
tract in which a constant buyout price pb is speciÔ¨Āed. If a type-S farmer accepts the contract, he
commits to cultivate the crop and sell it at pb to the SE in every period over a very long horizon.
It is reasonable to assume that a type-S farmer with a production cost c will accept the contract

could be diÔ¨Écult. First, na¬®ńĪve farmers may hesitate to make any long-term commitment, as they
likely Ô¨Ānd it diÔ¨Écult to assess its value over the long run. Furthermore, this contract may not seem

price, the farmers may not be willing to be locked in at the contract price.
4.3.5.
Numerical Comparison. We numerically compare the market price trajectories in

5.
ProÔ¨Āt-Driven Enterprise
While the important role of SEs and other social organizations in increasing farmers‚Äô welfare has
been widely acknowledged, one cannot expect their participation in every aspect of the agriculture
industry. In this section, we further study the commonly seen interactions between farmers and
a proÔ¨Āt-driven Ô¨Ārm who buys raw materials from upstream farmers for resale or may use these
materials to make products for sale. When all the farmers are na¬®ńĪve, we have seen that the price
uncertainty is a major obstacle to a stable stream of crop supply. Given supply uncertainty leads to
operational ineÔ¨Éciency, reducing the price risks and having a stable market supply may beneÔ¨Āt all
the stages along the supply chain. Therefore, it is often in the best interest of a proÔ¨Āt-driven Ô¨Ārm
to plan ahead to contract with farmers to ensure a safe and steady supply of agricultural products.
Given companies may not want to wait for actions taken by others, it is important to examine how
the market price risk and the resulting supply risk can be mitigated solely by a proÔ¨Āt-driven Ô¨Ārm.
SpeciÔ¨Ācally, we study the impact of a buyout contract oÔ¨Äered by a for-proÔ¨Āt Ô¨Ārm instead of a SE.
Suppose the Ô¨Ārm has a constant consumer demand d in each period. This demand d can also be
generally interpreted as a Ô¨Ārm‚Äôs target level of Ô¨Ānal product supply. Assume that one unit of crop
raw materials can be converted to one unit of Ô¨Ānal products. If the market supply qt ‚Č•d, the Ô¨Ārm
buys d units of raw materials from the market at the market price pt. If qt < d, the Ô¨Ārm has to buy
an additional quantity d ‚ąíqt from an external market at a higher price pm with pm > pt for any
t ‚Č•0. The external market is isolated from the focal market we have studied. The price diÔ¨Äerence
pm ‚ąípt can also be viewed as a penalty cost for those demands in excess of supply from the focal
market. We start with all farmers in the population being na¬®ńĪve farmers. Just like the SE in the
previous section, the Ô¨Ārm oÔ¨Äers a pre-season procurement contract at the beginning of each period
with a Ô¨Āxed price pb to őĪ fraction of na¬®ńĪve farmers. Like before, we refer to farmers who are oÔ¨Äered
the contract as type-S farmers and the rest as type-N farmers.

b+őĪ2 (¬Įp ‚ąí¬Įcd), and p2i‚Ä≤ has to be no lower than this threshold in
order to attract suÔ¨Écient type-N farmers, in addition to type-S farmers, to plant crops and meet
the total demand d in odd periods. A market price that is less than that threshold may cause
supply shortage and expensive sourcing from the external market in odd periods. Hence the Ô¨Ārm
has incentives to mitigate the market price Ô¨āuctuation to avoid such a low market price. However,
as discussed above the Ô¨Ārm does not have to completely eliminate the price Ô¨āuctuation. Therefore,
such a speculative behavior of the Ô¨Ārm would sustain the market price Ô¨āuctuation to some extent
and result in lower welfare of farmers than that under the contract with pb = ¬Įp.
6.
Extensions
The unstable market supply resulting from the price Ô¨āuctuations is often made worse by a host of
endogenous and exogenous causes. For example, farmers may choose which crop to plant among
 Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=2847477 
Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=2847477

27
multiple options based on their market prices, and this decision can be inÔ¨āuenced by crop produc-
tion yield, which is aÔ¨Äected by weather, insect pests, etc. In this section, we Ô¨Ārst extend Section
3 to a two-crops setting in which both na¬®ńĪve and strategic farmers choose to cultivate one of two
types of crops. In other words, the outside option of planting one crop for a farmer is endogenized
as the value of planting the other. We then further extend this model to capture the production
yield uncertainty. The same extensions can be naturally made to the settings in Sections 4 and 5.
Since the insights remain the same, for brevity we omit the similar analysis to these settings.
6.1.
Two Crops
We consider farmers that choose to plant one of two types of crops in each period. We use A and

distributed on [0, ¬Įc]. In addition to capturing the heterogeneity of farmers‚Äô production costs, the
assumed cost structure reÔ¨āects two plausible facts: Ô¨Ārst, each farmer may have diÔ¨Äerent production
costs for diÔ¨Äerent crops; second, no farmer has cost advantages over other farmers with both crops.
The analysis can be extended to settings with production costs in more general forms.

Proposition 14 shows that when farmers are able to choose one of two crops to plant in each
period, the market evolution is similar to the patterns in the single-crop model (see Proposition
1). When there is a lack of suÔ¨Écient number of strategic farmers, the market price process for each
crop does not converge and eventually alternates between two prices; when there is a suÔ¨Éciently
large number of strategic farmers, the price process of each crop will converge to ‚Ą¶‚ąíb
2, which is
independent of the segment size of strategic farmers, őĪ. Comparing with the single-crop model,
the key diÔ¨Äerence is that now the farmers‚Äô outside option of cultivating one crop is endogenized
as the value of cultivating the other crop. In particular, the high price of one crop at the end of a
period encourages na¬®ńĪve farmers to plant this crop in the next period, exacerbating the shortage
of the other crop. Hence, the market prices for two crops tend to wax and wane in alternation.
The limiting price ‚Ą¶‚ąíb
2 in Proposition 14(b) implies that when there are suÔ¨Écient strategic
farmers, eventually half of the farmers with the cost advantage for crop A, i.e., c < ¬Įc
2, always
cultivate crop A and the other farmers always cultivate crop B in each period. Farmers are divided
exactly in half, because the potential market sizes of both crops are assumed to be the same as ‚Ą¶.
It can be shown that, when the two crops have diÔ¨Äerent market sizes, the market price of each crop
will still converge as long as: (i) there are enough strategic farmers and (ii) the diÔ¨Äerence between
the market sizes of the two crops is not too large. In the long run, the crop with a larger market
size attracts more than half of the farmers. In terms of the farmers‚Äô welfare, the same statement
as in Proposition 3 still holds in the two-crops model, because strategic farmers continue to help
stabilize the market prices for both crops. That is, our Ô¨Ānding about strategic farmers‚Äô socially
beneÔ¨Ācial rationality continues to hold when there are two crops.
6.2.
Random Yield
Now we extend the previous two-crops model by considering yield uncertainty in farmers‚Äô harvest.
This uncertainty, which is attributed to uncontrollable external factors (e.g., weather, water supply,
pests), captures the fact that the harvest quantity is often diÔ¨Äerent from the quantity actually
planted. We refer to the former as the random yield and the latter as the production quantity. We
capture yield uncertainty by the ratio of the random yield to the production quantity, and assume
that the yield uncertainty in period t for all crops in the same district is the same, denoted by ő≥t.

7.
Conclusion
In developing countries, price Ô¨āuctuation in agricultural markets is blocking the path to prosperity
for small-scale farmers. Although the burgeoning literature studies the value of disclosing market
information for price discovery and how that aÔ¨Äects farmers‚Äô welfare, little attention has been
paid to farmers‚Äô heterogeneous capability in obtaining and processing the market information.
In reality, only farmers who are educated and not in remote areas may obtain and utilize the
market information in their decision making. It thus gives rise to the concern whether eÔ¨Äorts to
disseminate market information treat all farmers fairly and whether the market is evolving in a
socially desirable way.
In this paper, we oÔ¨Äer a stylized multi-period model to study farmers‚Äô dynamic crop-planting
decisions and their impact on the market prices. We consider both na¬®ńĪve farmers who react short-
sightedly to the latest market price, and strategic farmers who are able to collect and utilize
market information to anticipate rationally the near-future market price. Our model builds a
micro-foundation to capture the root cause of the price Ô¨āuctuations observed in many agricultural
markets. That is, because na¬®ńĪve farmers base their crop planting decisions on the latest market
price, they cyclically herd in planting the crop, thus leading to recurring over-production and price
slashing. We Ô¨Ānd that the forward-looking behavior of strategic farmers could alleviate the herding
behavior, therefore being both rational and socially beneÔ¨Ācial in that it can stabilize the market
price and increase all focal farmers‚Äô welfare. However, to sustain this notion of ‚Äúsocially beneÔ¨Ācial
rationality,‚ÄĚ there must be a suÔ¨Écient number of strategic farmers to collectively move the needle.
Another major Ô¨Ānding of this paper is that even when it fails to cultivate strategic farmers,
both social entrepreneurs and for-proÔ¨Āt Ô¨Ārms can beneÔ¨Āt all focal farmers as well as themselves
by oÔ¨Äering a pre-season buyout contract to a fraction of farmers. Those farmers then only need to
base their crop decisions on the price oÔ¨Äered by the Ô¨Ārm. However, although a carefully designed
contract beneÔ¨Āts all focal farmers who have no cost advantage and leads to smaller income gaps
among farmers, a non-socially-optimal designed contract may reÔ¨āect a social entrepreneur‚Äôs over-
subsidy tendency, or a for-proÔ¨Āt Ô¨Ārm‚Äôs speculative incentive to mitigate but not eliminate the
market price Ô¨āuctuation, preventing farmers from achieving the most welfare. We also extend our
model to account for multiple crops and yield uncertainty. We Ô¨Ānd that our results from the base
model still hold qualitatively in those extensions.

B.
Backward-Looking Farmers
In this extension, we assume farmers lack full information (e.g., ‚Ą¶, őĪ and F(¬∑)) and thus cannot
use (3) to rationally anticipate the near-future market price pt. Instead, in each period strategic
farmers predict the market price on the basis of the average price in the last two periods, i.e.,
ňÜpt =

Meindl 2013, Chapter 7), as it is easier to obtain the necessary information about historical prices
than future prices. The strategic farmers‚Äô perceived utility becomes

surplus than the naive farmers with the same production cost.

Lemma G.3. Suppose Assumptions (1)-(5) hold and pb < ¬Įp. Then the price process does not
converge.
Proof of Lemma G.3.
Whenever the low price in each cycle falls into the range of [pb, ¬Įp] which
is a necessary process for convergence, the contract is not eÔ¨Äective anymore and thus the price
process will stop converging because the price does not converge when b ‚Č•¬Įc in the model with
naive farmers only and no contract. Therefore, the price does not converge if pb < ¬Įp.
‚Ė°
Proof of Proposition 5
The result follows immediately by combining Lemmas G.1, G.2, and
G.3.

Proof of Proposition 10.
Lemma I.1 implies that the inÔ¨Ānite horizon problem can be viewed as
an inÔ¨Ānite number of independent problem, each of which consists of one cycle (two periods), and
Lemma I.2 gives the optimal solution of each independent problem. Moreover, Lemma I.3 shows
that with the optimal operation, the prices will be closer to the market limiting price in the current
cycle if they are closer to the market limiting price in the last cycle. Combing the three lemmas,
we arrive the conclusion.
